    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6971206855866462210">Jean-Paul Sartre, the great French existentialist, displays his mastery of drama in NO EXIT, an unforgettable portrayal of hell.<br/>The play is a depiction of the afterlife in which three deceased characters are punished by being locked into a room together for all eternity. It is the source of Sartre's especially famous and often misinterpreted quotation "L'enfer, c'est les a</span>
  <span id="freeText6971206855866462210" style="display:none">Jean-Paul Sartre, the great French existentialist, displays his mastery of drama in NO EXIT, an unforgettable portrayal of hell.<br />The play is a depiction of the afterlife in which three deceased characters are punished by being locked into a room together for all eternity. It is the source of Sartre's especially famous and often misinterpreted quotation "L'enfer, c'est les autres" or "Hell is other people", a reference to Sartre's ideas about the Look and the perpetual ontological struggle of being caused to see oneself as an object in the world of another consciousness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6971206855866462210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2024349089" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2024349089" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2024349089?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=plays-theatre">plays-theatre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2024349089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18131055604565992834">The mere abstraction of 'forever' makes me shiver, but throw in that word 'hell', and the heat starts to rise to an unprecedented level, whether bliss or burning to be subjected to anything forever, with no foreseeable or even imaginable end is almost impossible to comprehend. I would promptly take living multiple lives briefly, than a single one indefinitely. As reality outside of any timeline doesn't seem to carry with it any kind of need for initiative, for good nor evil. <br/><br/>"Hm! So here we are?</span>
  <span id="freeText18131055604565992834" style="display:none">The mere abstraction of 'forever' makes me shiver, but throw in that word 'hell', and the heat starts to rise to an unprecedented level, whether bliss or burning to be subjected to anything forever, with no foreseeable or even imaginable end is almost impossible to comprehend. I would promptly take living multiple lives briefly, than a single one indefinitely. As reality outside of any timeline doesn't seem to carry with it any kind of need for initiative, for good nor evil. <br /><br />"Hm! So here we are?"<br /><br />As with most of Sartre's work, he seeks to identify the various tenets commonly associated with existentialism, and 'No Exit' is widely regarded as the literary expression of another Sartrean work, 'Being and Nothingness', published in the same year.<br />The thing I found most unsettling about this play was not the play itself, but the fact Sartre wrote with only 'one act' in mind, simply as to not piss off the Germans by keeping theatergoers there after the imposed curfew, this was 1944, with many forms of entertainment going through approval by German censors, prior to the liberation of Paris.<br /><br />Garcin, Estelle, Inez, three souls, all whisked away to that doomed place of fire and brimstone, there's no escaping each other, trapped in a room, and there's no escaping the truth. These characters torture each other as they are able to reflect each other better than any mirror, they also torture one another because of what they can't have. Inez wants Estelle, but Estelle wants Garcin, Garcin sides with Inez, creating a three-way non-love affair. Just dealing with other people is hell enough, who needs, torture devices, molten lava, or perpetual darkness, emotional pain takes the brunt of the suffering.<br /><br />I was thinking along the lines of a dark, surreal Kafkaesque nightmare, but it's nothing of the sort really. Here, this is much more of a quirky and light-hearted rendition of hell, Sartre's saying something that's very true to human nature, pushing this idea to its extreme, but using hell as a metaphor for the actions of the three individuals.<br />Ironically, the play starts with forever, and ends with forever.....<br /><br />Looking back on the past is a form of torture, particularly when we fail to choose an act when the opportunity presents itself. When alive man can always choose, to rearrange order, before lifelong events are frozen into the void, where they will reside on a permanent basis.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18131055604565992834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_630614376" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/630614376" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/630614376?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19662189-no-exit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer630614376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16902000901201346452">Huis Clos = No Exit, Jean-Paul Sartre<br/><br/>No Exit is a 1944 existentialist French play by Jean-Paul Sartre. The original title is the French equivalent of the legal term in camera, referring to a private discussion behind closed doors. <br/><br/>The play begins with three characters who find themselves waiting in a mysterious room. It is a depiction of the afterlife in which three deceased characters are punished by being locked into a room together for eternity. <br/><br/>عنوانها: اتاق بسته؛ در بسته، خلوتکده؛ دوزخ؛ تار</span>
  <span id="freeText16902000901201346452" style="display:none">Huis Clos = No Exit, Jean-Paul Sartre<br /><br />No Exit is a 1944 existentialist French play by Jean-Paul Sartre. The original title is the French equivalent of the legal term in camera, referring to a private discussion behind closed doors. <br /><br />The play begins with three characters who find themselves waiting in a mysterious room. It is a depiction of the afterlife in which three deceased characters are punished by being locked into a room together for eternity. <br /><br />عنوانها: اتاق بسته؛ در بسته، خلوتکده؛ دوزخ؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و هفتم ماه می سال 2003میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: خلوتکده نمایشنامه در یک پرده؛ اثر: ژان پل سارتر؛ مترجم قاسم صنعوی؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، کتاب پارسه، 1389، در 79ص، شابک 9786005026863؛ موضوع: نمایشنامه های نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: در بسته؛ مترجم: ناصر غیاثی، به صورت ای.بوک<br /><br />عنوان: در بسته، مترجم: زهرا جعفری؛ تهران، هفت پیکر، در 71ص؛<br /><br />عنوان: در بسته، مترجم: سعید عجم حسنی، تهران، مرغ آمین، 1381، در 85ص؛<br /><br />عنوان: دوزخ، مترجم: سعید عجم حسنی، 1381، در 85ص؛<br /><br />عنوان: اتاق بسته، مترجم: امید جمشیدی، تهران، افراز، 1393، در 72ص؛ <br /><br />نمایشنامه ی «خلوتکده» با عنوانهای: «در بسته»، تهران، هفت پیکر، با ترجمه زهرا جعفری؛ در 71ص؛ و با ترجمه سعید عجم حسنی، تهران، مرغ آمین، 1381، در 85ص، با عنوان «اتاق بسته» با ترجمه امید جمشیدی، تهران، افراز، 1393، در 72ص؛ و با عنوان «در بسته دوزخ، با ترجمه سعید عجم حسنی در 85ص» و با عنوان «دوزخ، با ترجمه: مصطفی فرزانه، و عبدالحسین نوشین؛ و با ترجمه حمید سمندریان، در 56ص؛ و با ترجمه علی سلامی، در 102ص» نيز ترجمه و منتشر شده است<br /><br />نمایشنامه‌ ای یک پرده‌ ای، و یکی از معروفترین آثار نمایشی «ژان پل سارتر» است؛ شخصیت‌های نمایشنامه، با مرگ‌هایی متفاوت، از دنیا رفته‌ اند، و حال در یکجا گرد آمده‌ اند؛ کار آنان، نمایش عذاب‌های ضمیر انسان‌های محروم از آزادی ست؛ <br /><br />نمایشنامه‌، چهار شخصیت دارد، که سه تن از آن‌ها اصلی هستند و نفر چهارم مردی ست، که با عنوان پیشخدمت خوانده می‌شود؛ سه شخصیت اصلی این نمایشنامه، همانند سه ضلع یک مثلث، که در عین ارتباط، بر همدیگر نیز تاثیر می‌گذارند؛ دو زن «استل» و «اینس»؛ و دو مرد یکی به نام «گارسَن»، و شخصیت دیگری که با عنوان «پیشخدمت» خوانده می‌شود، صحنه ی نمایشنامه اتاقی ست تزئین شده، به سبک امپراتوری دوم فرانسه، که «استل»، «اینس» و «گارسَن»، پس از مرگ، و در دوزخ، یکدیگر را پیدا می‌کنند؛<br /><br />سارتر، در این نمایشنامه، با استادی موقعیتی را تدارک دیده، تا شخصیت‌ها، در هراس از عاقبتی که در انتظار آن‌هاست، به مرورِ گذشته ی خود، بپردازند؛ شاید بهترین نمایشنامه «سارتر» نباشد اما از جنبه‌ هایی مهم‌ترین نمایشنامه ی ایشان است؛<br /><br />ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_2680474990" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2680474990" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2680474990?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2680474990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10805041191036481841">I DON’T CARE WHAT THEY SAY<br/>I WON’T STAY<br/>IN A WORLD WITHOUT LOVE!<br/>- Peter & Gordon, 1963<br/><br/>But what if you didn’t have a CHOICE?<br/><br/>So says Jean-Paul Sartre in this play that is so close to the bone - and so close to theological orthodoxy - that we’re actually AMAZED to find it’s written by an atheist.<br/><br/>Three people, together for all Eternity. Three pathologically angry, anti-social people. <br/><br/>And this is their shared Hell. <br/><br/>Their forever home.<br/><br/>And you know what else? Forever after, they “can‘t get no satisfactio</span>
  <span id="freeText10805041191036481841" style="display:none">I DON’T CARE WHAT THEY SAY<br />I WON’T STAY<br />IN A WORLD WITHOUT LOVE!<br />- Peter &amp; Gordon, 1963<br /><br />But what if you didn’t have a CHOICE?<br /><br />So says Jean-Paul Sartre in this play that is so close to the bone - and so close to theological orthodoxy - that we’re actually AMAZED to find it’s written by an atheist.<br /><br />Three people, together for all Eternity. Three pathologically angry, anti-social people. <br /><br />And this is their shared Hell. <br /><br />Their forever home.<br /><br />And you know what else? Forever after, they “can‘t get no satisfaction!”<br /><br />Because they’re ALL sexually incompatible with one another.<br /><br />It’s not surprising the biggest takeaway one-liner from this work is ‘Hell is other people’ - for there’s now NO EXIT from each others’ ruthless and judgmental surveillance!<br /><br />But is this oft-quoted maxim true for ALL of us?<br /><br />You, know, if it ISN’T true for us - for not all of us can sympathize with Sartre’s unrepentant despair - we’ll probably never have to share this trio’s ugly fate!<br /><br />Our love for each other and for life’s simple joys will save us.<br /><br />Just like it was for the remarkable writers that emerged from the prisons, the ‘work camps’, the gas chambers of the twentieth century - writers like Nien Cheng, Ernest Gordon, and Elie Wiesel - who had enough faith and love left to REBUILD their lives.<br /><br />For their love had been endlessly refined in a crucible of Fire.<br /><br />And they had protected their deep humanity, for to them, it was sacred.<br /><br />The devil may always get his due - and his endlessly various ways with us - but for a loving heart that endures in spite of his torture, like these faithful hearts, hell is never permanent.<br /><br />For these are the ones who in this world - and the next - continuously escape the damnation of despair, unlike the postmodernists who exalt their personal angst and disquiet to a godly status.<br /><br />For those unfortunates, like Sartre, there may NEVER be any rest.<br /><br />And poor old Sartre, eternally avoiding the Other, could never - for reasons of pride - muster up the strength to truly ‘face the face’ of this Other in shared Love and respect.<br /><br />And simple warm humanity.<br /><br />He had made his decision, and his hurt pride called it irrevocable.<br /><br />So for him a hellish conclusion was inevitable.<br /><br />But hopefully it will never be so for us...<br /><br />If we always let love and hope guide us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10805041191036481841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2481664904" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2481664904" />
    <a title="JV (semi-hiatus)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus"><img alt="JV (semi-hiatus)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559624981p2/20906165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2481664904?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JV (semi-hiatus)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JV (semi-hiatus)" href="/user/show/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus">JV (semi-hiatus)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=curiouser-and-curiouser">curiouser-and-curiouser</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2481664904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10960914599290528635">A single room with Second Empire furniture (no mirrors, no windows, three sofas, one paper knife, lights that won't go off), and no torturer. Accompanied by a mysterious valet, three incredulous characters (Inez Serrano, Estelle Rigault, and Joseph Garci) are escorted inside and are eternally locked away within its walls. At first, no one wants to admit what each has committed to deserve this damnation, but as the play progresses, they confess their crimes — their deepest, darkest secrets expose</span>
  <span id="freeText10960914599290528635" style="display:none">A single room with Second Empire furniture (no mirrors, no windows, three sofas, one paper knife, lights that won't go off), and no torturer. Accompanied by a mysterious valet, three incredulous characters (Inez Serrano, Estelle Rigault, and Joseph Garci) are escorted inside and are eternally locked away within its walls. At first, no one wants to admit what each has committed to deserve this damnation, but as the play progresses, they confess their crimes — their deepest, darkest secrets exposed and pretences stripped off.<blockquote>
  <i>"There were days when you peered into yourself, into the secret places of your heart, and what you saw there made you faint with horror. And then, next day, you didn't know what to make of it, you couldn't interpret the horror you had glimpsed the day before. Yes, you know what evil costs."</i>
</blockquote>Without any mirrors, they are forced to rely on each other. This is the gaze at which they see themselves through each others' eyes.<blockquote>
  <i>"Inez: Sit down. Come closer. Closer. Look into my eyes. What do you see?<br>Estelle: Oh, I'm there! But so tiny I can't see myself properly.<br>Inez: But I can. Every inch of you."</i>
</blockquote>By becoming human mirrors in which they can pass moral judgment based on one's past actions (or past existences) and being an object under scrutiny through someone's eyes, they relentlessly torture one another psychologically through their vile interactions, bringing out the truth and the worst qualities in each of them.<blockquote>
  <i>"Alone, none of us can save himself or herself; we're linked together inextricably."</i>
</blockquote>Being stuck with the people you abhor the most in the afterlife is dreadful! And that, for me, is the worst kind of suffering that I could never endure. The mind is really a fragile one, and exposing me to that kind of infernal nightmare is morbidly horrifying!<blockquote>
  <i>"HELL IS — OTHER PEOPLE!"<i></i></i>
</blockquote>While the above quote might sound misanthropic, it actually isn't in the context of this play. What makes it "hell" is that if our relations or interactions with other people eventually come to a point where everything boils down to having a toxic, destructive relationship, being objectified/humiliated by others, now that is hell! <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1533611955ra/26089175.gif" width="300" height="168" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/123933.No_Exit" title="No Exit by Jean-Paul Sartre" rel="nofollow noopener">No Exit</a> is a great examination of human nature and our desire to be admired. If you've experienced hell, then heed Margaret Atwood's advice: <b>
  <i>nolite te bastardes carborundorum</i>
</b>! Or better yet, be like Cersei Lannister. Blow those bastards to smithereens and relish your cinematic masterpiece! <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1533611955ra/26089176.gif" width="300" height="166" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1533611955ra/26089177.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And if you're already an "absentee" in the physical world, you might want to get crazy and grab that paper knife in that claustrophobic room for stabbing your foe in the afterlife. But then again, you're already "dead"! So what's the point? And as Garcin would say, "Well, well, let's get on with it…" And so the torment begins again...</span>
  <a data-text-id="10960914599290528635" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1130688427" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130688427" />
    <a title="Forrest" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811928-forrest"><img alt="Forrest" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558621304p2/811928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130688427?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Forrest" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Forrest" href="/user/show/811928-forrest">Forrest</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130688427" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8072494630469362743">As a function of pure entertainment, Sartre's <i>No Exit</i> is brilliant. Ironically, Sartre uses almost-pure dialogue to "show not tell" the dilemma faced by Garcin, Inez, and Estelle, three "absentees" (a euphemism for "the dead") locked into a room, condemned to be together for eternity. Each has arrived here for different reasons, but all three possess qualities that bring out the worst in the others. Rather than the traditional hellish tropes of horned demons and hell-fire, this play evokes more </span>
  <span id="freeText8072494630469362743" style="display:none">As a function of pure entertainment, Sartre's <i>No Exit</i> is brilliant. Ironically, Sartre uses almost-pure dialogue to "show not tell" the dilemma faced by Garcin, Inez, and Estelle, three "absentees" (a euphemism for "the dead") locked into a room, condemned to be together for eternity. Each has arrived here for different reasons, but all three possess qualities that bring out the worst in the others. Rather than the traditional hellish tropes of horned demons and hell-fire, this play evokes more special tortures - the ability to see into the world of the living only long enough to know what other living beings are saying about you since you've been "absent," the pressure of having three personalities who delight in interfering with each potential pairing (and the promise of solace that might mean), and the exquisite pains of honest self-doubt.<br /><br />I had hoped to take this as a "primary source" of Sartre's philosophy, but perhaps I've got it all backwards. I am familiar with the tenets of existentialism that Sartre espoused, but the philosophical gloss that is given in <i>No Exit</i> seems to be as much a veneer as a core underpinning. <br /><br />That said, one need not think too hard to realize that this is an excruciatingly uncomfortable examination of human nature in all its banality. The characters at once seem likable, or at least their character flaws seem excusable, initially. As the play goes on, though, we begin to see each person's flaws magnified, as with a glass, until the full impact of their crimes and selfishness are realized. Soon, the audience feels shame for having excused or even liked the absentees, with a full realization that any of them (the audience, that is) could be seen as Garcin, Inez, or Estelle, or possibly even a conglomeration of any two or all three. <br /><br />Not for those who don't like looking in the mirror. Or even for those who do. Prepare to be discomfited!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8072494630469362743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1721697815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1721697815" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721697815?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=4-europe">4-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=sad-lonely-thinking-of-suicide">sad-lonely-thinking-of-suicide</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bestest">bestest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721697815" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2664351222685993846"><b>
  <u>Two is Company, Three is Hell<u></u></u>
</b><br/><br/>Oh my sweet Satan! this is one hell of a play. This definitely is scariest vision of inferno, and the vision is just too simple:<br/><blockquote>
  <br/>
  <i> "You re-member all we were told about the torture-chambers, the fire and brimstone, the "burning marl." Old wives' tales! There's no need for red-hot pokers. Hell is—other people!"</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>And who can be better torturer than brains behind holocausts and Abu-Gharibs?<br/><blockquote>
  <i><br/>“As for me, I am mean: that means that I need the suffering of others t</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2664351222685993846" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>Two is Company, Three is Hell<u></u></u>
</b><br /><br />Oh my sweet Satan! this is one hell of a play. This definitely is scariest vision of inferno, and the vision is just too simple:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i> "You re-member all we were told about the torture-chambers, the fire and brimstone, the "burning marl." Old wives' tales! There's no need for red-hot pokers. Hell is—other people!"</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />And who can be better torturer than brains behind holocausts and Abu-Gharibs?<br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />“As for me, I am mean: that means that I need the suffering of others to exist. A flame. A flame in their hearts. When I am all alone, I am extinguished.”<br /></i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Just as even the bigesst loners among us need some sort of company every once in a while, each one of us need some time alone, all by oneself. The three characters of the play are struck in a locked room where lights never go off and there is no question of sleeping. The dis-likable nature of these people, the uncertainty as to what punishments are to follow and visions of what is being said about them in their absence (after their death) is enough to create a tension that leads to differences between them, making them each other's nemesis. But what would you do if you have to live, okay correction, stay forever with two of your foes and none of you can kill each other? <br /><br />You may argue that it is their inability to co-operate which created lead to those quarrels but really, being constantly seen by each other means that sooner or later you should find something to fight about - and, unlike while living, once you started fighting you can't have a moment alone or listen to music or something to calm down. The only item left in the place is a pen-knife, which is useless except to ensure that these people know how they hate each other by trying to stab each-other. Perfect. Forget Biblical authors, forget Hindu pundits, forget old wives, forget John Krammer, forget Dante; Devil himself needs to take lectures from Sartre.<br /><br />Also one can't help but love the way Sartre keeps things simple whether it is time moving faster in hell (thus cutting drama to one scene) or fact that visions from Earth are narrated by character in question (instead of showing actual scenes - limiting play to only four characters and a single place). Genuis!<br /><br />P. S. Now I know from where they got the idea for 'Big Brother'.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2664351222685993846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2657355535" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Gary Inbinder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5963850-gary-inbinder"><img alt="Gary Inbinder" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442496373p2/5963850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2657355535?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary Inbinder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary Inbinder" href="/user/show/5963850-gary-inbinder">Gary Inbinder</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5963850-gary-inbinder?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5963850-gary-inbinder?shelf=satire">satire</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2657355535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11152348774756246151">L'enfer, c'est les autres" or "Hell is other people. Imagine being locked in a small room for eternity with a group of people you don't like and/or who don't like you. Is that hell? Maybe. It certainly wouldn't be fun. But there's more to it than that. Do we exist without other people? Do they objectify us? Do they define us? Are we just characters in someone else's play? At least it's a point of departure for a discussion. <br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_972735740" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/972735740" />
    <a title="Vipassana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13430088-vipassana"><img alt="Vipassana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546371963p2/13430088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/972735740?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vipassana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vipassana" href="/user/show/13430088-vipassana">Vipassana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13430088-vipassana?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13430088-vipassana?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13430088-vipassana?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer972735740" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8791429949460979735"><i>Hell is other people!</i><br/><br/>That statement is beyond doubt the most famous of Satre's. However, it is only after one is acquainted with No Exit does one realise how grossly misinterpreted that statement is. In the context of this play, the statement is not misanthropic. It highlights a symptom of the condition that is life. Perhaps this quote, where Estelle peers into the eyes of Inez for the lack of a mirror, characterizes Satre's iconic quote better.<br/><br/><i>Your scare me rather. My reflection in the glass ne</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8791429949460979735" style="display:none"><i>Hell is other people!</i><br /><br />That statement is beyond doubt the most famous of Satre's. However, it is only after one is acquainted with No Exit does one realise how grossly misinterpreted that statement is. In the context of this play, the statement is not misanthropic. It highlights a symptom of the condition that is life. Perhaps this quote, where Estelle peers into the eyes of Inez for the lack of a mirror, characterizes Satre's iconic quote better.<br /><br /><i>Your scare me rather. My reflection in the glass never did that; of course, I knew it so well. Like something I had tamed...I'm going to smile, and my smile will sink down into your pupils, and heaven knows what it will become.</i> <br /><br />We see ourselves as a reflection of the perception of others. Often we cultivate the face that we present to them as well. Yet, the other people don't receive this image as we present it. The brush of a few layers, and see what we fear of ourselves. The denial of that moment is strong. It makes one defensive. But how long can you defend your self? It is absolute hell to live with one's guard up all the time. Though if one were to go through such a situation, I wonder how long it would last. It would appear pointless to go on forever. Does the will to hold up a crumbling mask last for long? "<i>A man is what he wills himself to be</i>", as Garcin says. <br /><br />As a little thought experiment, I wonder what happens in this hell beyond the limits of this story. The end leads me to believe it might not be that hellish after all.<br /><br />This is the first piece of Satre's that I have read. Due to several reasons, I did not expect to enjoy his work much. After reading this play, I look forward to them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8791429949460979735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1662267050" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1662267050" />
    <a title="Brian Yahn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22849115-brian-yahn"><img alt="Brian Yahn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464719293p2/22849115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1662267050?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian Yahn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian Yahn" href="/user/show/22849115-brian-yahn">Brian Yahn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1662267050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7379879801249263015">The gist of No Exit is this: three people are put in a room. The interesting thing about the room is, it's Hell. And what's really interesting about Hell is -- there's no torture devices, no devouring flames, it's just a trio of insufferable people left to themselves.<br/><br/>The premise is pretty genius. The setting, a Second Empire drawing room, a tastelessly opulent room basically panders to me. It seems like Jean-Paul Sartre is using the setting as a statement, to say something is wrong with the Bour</span>
  <span id="freeText7379879801249263015" style="display:none">The gist of No Exit is this: three people are put in a room. The interesting thing about the room is, it's Hell. And what's really interesting about Hell is -- there's no torture devices, no devouring flames, it's just a trio of insufferable people left to themselves.<br /><br />The premise is pretty genius. The setting, a Second Empire drawing room, a tastelessly opulent room basically panders to me. It seems like Jean-Paul Sartre is using the setting as a statement, to say something is wrong with the Bourgeoisie, and that something is everything about them. In the beginning, he had me hooked with the idea of the room. Then he had me hooked with the people in it--why these people? Why are they in Hell?<br /><br />And yet, I didn't love this play. I didn't love the characters. I didn't see why putting them together in a room was Hell. Jean-Paul Sartre didn't show me that. It seemed like he just told me so, and that was my suspension of disbelief.<br /><br />That being said, <b>this is the only book I've ever read in one day</b>. So it was great. It has some beautiful imagery and amazing dialogue for a translation. Something I'll quote to the end of times is: "I'm a crowd all by myself."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7379879801249263015" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1172263251991758290">This play gave me a wonderful idea for my next photography project and another view of hell. Every time I pick up something by existentialist authors, I’m reminded of how much they closely border upon my thoughts on life.   <span id="freeText1172263251991758290" style="display:none">This play gave me a wonderful idea for my next photography project and another view of hell. Every time I pick up something by existentialist authors, I’m reminded of how much they closely border upon my thoughts on life. The play centers around three characters who are trapped in a room after being dead for several hours and begin conversing about each other’s idea of why they’ve landed there which they think is essentially the hell. They bicker, fight and hate each other and we come to see how each one of them is a cause of torment for another. <br />So to sum it up <b>
  <i> “Hell is other people.” </i>
</b><br />There was never a time while reading this I wasn’t reminded of The Breakfast Club. The bickering and incessant probing for truth from each character was done almost alike. Even the lipstick scene is pretty much the same! Hughes, like Satre, tried to steer the focus away from stereotypical images and presented what is human under the façade. The play is short and quick to read but leaves a much lasting impression forever, and ever, and ever…<br /></span>

<div id="review_296823110" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/296823110" />
    <a title="Olivia Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6582724-olivia-thomas"><img alt="Olivia Thomas" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/296823110?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia Thomas" href="/user/show/6582724-olivia-thomas">Olivia Thomas</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer296823110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3807923240964748006">We've often heard the cliche "Hell is other people", but no one has ever taken the saying more literally than Jean-Paul Sartre in 'No Exit'. The play opens on Garcin, a man damned for eternity. He is lead to a plain room with no mirrors or beds, only three couches and poor decorating. Like the two women that arrive after him, Inez and Estelle, he wonders why there are no torturing devices in Hell as expected. However, once the three start in conversation, they realize that they have been chosen </span>
  <span id="freeText3807923240964748006" style="display:none">We've often heard the cliche "Hell is other people", but no one has ever taken the saying more literally than Jean-Paul Sartre in 'No Exit'. The play opens on Garcin, a man damned for eternity. He is lead to a plain room with no mirrors or beds, only three couches and poor decorating. Like the two women that arrive after him, Inez and Estelle, he wonders why there are no torturing devices in Hell as expected. However, once the three start in conversation, they realize that they have been chosen to torture each other. Though one might assume they do this by annoying and angering each other, this is only a fraction of their ability to make the other two suffer. What really proves to be torturous is how they break down each other's facades and lies, and make them examine their own unacceptable failings. Upon arrival, each is unwilling to admit why they were damned or offer any honesty about their former lives on Earth. Each character fears judgement from the other two, and therefore attempts to hide their failings, even from themselves. The absence of mirrors in the room intensifies this fear by symbolically not allowing each character to judge themselves, but only be seen by others. Garcin's cowardice, Inez's vindictiveness, and Estelle's vanity are all revealed by the end of the play. All three have defined their own characteristics in life, and find it unbearable to take responsibility for their choices. Resisting their own ability to define themselves, they rely on their roommates to judge them and point out the flaws they have created themselves. The conflicting fear of others' judgement and self-judgement is the torture they all must face. Almost everyone can relate to this conflict. We find it difficult to be honest with ourselves about our shortcomings, even though we create them ourselves. In addition, feeling judged by others is can cause insecurity in even the most confident person. It all comes down to how we let ourselves be defined: either at our own hand or those around us. Existentialist works of literature, such as The Stranger by Albert Camus, argue that we choose our own paths and must take responsibility for them in order to achieve peace within. If the characters in 'No Exit' could only realize that they have determined their own fates and accept that they alone have the right to define who they are, "other people" might not be so torturous after all. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3807923240964748006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer117087533801579358">I read No Exit in English and French and saw the play while studying in France. In par with Sartre's idea of freedom, we create heaven and hell today, in this life on earth, by other's judgments of our acts. "Hell is the other", the phrase encapsulating heaven and hell, I found quite to the point. The 'other's' eyes pierce into my head, judging and shaping who I am, which I must escape, through my freedom to choose. No Exit is a simple and yet powerful play, which defined Petan's (northeren) Fra</span>
  <span id="freeText117087533801579358" style="display:none">I read No Exit in English and French and saw the play while studying in France. In par with Sartre's idea of freedom, we create heaven and hell today, in this life on earth, by other's judgments of our acts. "Hell is the other", the phrase encapsulating heaven and hell, I found quite to the point. The 'other's' eyes pierce into my head, judging and shaping who I am, which I must escape, through my freedom to choose. No Exit is a simple and yet powerful play, which defined Petan's (northeren) France, giving in to Hitler and his thugs, who marched into Paris on the conscience of France's red carpet. It is no surprise that eventually the greatness of France gave into shame, turning into bitterness and empty pride.</span>

  <span id="freeText17825019845204017084" style="display:none">Quite similar in tone and temper to his collection The Wall, No Exit successfully burrowed itself into the collective unconscious: likely tens of millions of people have never heard of Jean-Paul Sartre but have heard the notion that Hell—is other people. <div id="review_3268194987" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3268194987" />
    <a title="Cody Sexton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3452713-cody-sexton"><img alt="Cody Sexton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556077120p2/3452713.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3268194987?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody Sexton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody Sexton" href="/user/show/3452713-cody-sexton">Cody Sexton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3268194987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16052096241991679986">The idea of being trapped in a room with two strangers and no toothbrushes is not an especially appealing premise. But if this condition is made eternal, then it suddenly becomes sheer torture.<br/>This is the setting of existentialist philosopher Jean-Paul Sartre’s most famous play, No Exit (Huis Clos), first performed in May 1944. The play features three characters, Garcin, Inèz, and Estelle: whom, after their deaths, are escorted by a mysterious Valet to the same room in Hell and locked inside. Wh</span>
  <span id="freeText16052096241991679986" style="display:none">The idea of being trapped in a room with two strangers and no toothbrushes is not an especially appealing premise. But if this condition is made eternal, then it suddenly becomes sheer torture.<br />This is the setting of existentialist philosopher Jean-Paul Sartre’s most famous play, No Exit (Huis Clos), first performed in May 1944. The play features three characters, Garcin, Inèz, and Estelle: whom, after their deaths, are escorted by a mysterious Valet to the same room in Hell and locked inside. While they had expected all sorts of instruments of suffering, they are taken aback when they find themselves in a drawing room adorned in Second Empire elegance. This style recalls a sense of order, of tastefulness, of bourgeois decorum that contrasts with the characters’ sinful crimes. At first, none of them wants to confess the reason for their damnation, nor do they understand why there seems to be a lack of physical pain. Slowly, however, they eventually come to realize that they are actually supposed to be each other’s agents of torment.<br />After some argument, they decide to confess their crimes, just so they know what to expect from each other. Garcin cheated on and mistreated his wife, and was executed by firing squad for desertion; Inèz is a manipulative and misandrist sadist who seduced her cousin's wife, Florence, while living with them - which drove the cousin to kill himself, and resulted in Florence asphyxiating herself and Inèz by flooding the room with gas while they slept, out of guilt. While Estelle had an affair and then killed the resulting child, prompting the child's father to commit suicide. Despite their revelations, they continue to perturb one another.<br />Garcin finally begins giving in to the lascivious Estelle's escalating attempts to seduce him, which drives Inèz hysterical. Garcin is constantly interrupted by his own guilt, however, and begs Estelle to tell him he is not a coward for attempting to flee his country during wartime. While she complies, Inèz mockingly tells him that Estelle is just feigning attraction to him so that she can be with a man - any man. This causes Garcin to abruptly attempt an escape. After trying to open the door repeatedly, it inexplicably and abruptly opens, but he is unable to bring himself to leave, and the others remain as well. He says that he will not be saved until he can convince Inèz that he is not a coward. She refuses, saying that he is obviously a coward, and promises to make him miserable forever. Estelle tries to persevere in her seduction of Garcin, but he says that he cannot make love while Inèz is watching. Estelle, infuriated, picks up a paper knife and repeatedly stabs Inèz. Inèz chides Estelle, saying that they are all already dead, and even furiously stabs herself to prove that point. As Estelle begins to laugh hysterically at the idea of them being dead and trapped together forever, the others join in a prolonged fit of laughter before Garcin finally concludes, "Eh bien, continuons..." ("Well then, let's get on with it...").<br />Yet, it’s the play’s most famous line, “Hell is other people” (“L’Enfer, c’est les autres”), uttered once Garcin realizes that there will never be any escape from being looked upon by Inez and Estelle, that thoroughly captures Sartre’s existentialism: it encapsulates the idea that we constantly feel scrutinized by others, and that the perception other people have of us is crucial in shaping the way we think about ourselves. Making, No Exit, in effect, a play about the "devouring" gaze of the Other and how it restricts our freedom.<br />Sartre had a tremendous abhorrence for the gaze of others. Because it is through the gaze of others that we are reminded that our validation and identity derives from this very gaze. Sartre claims that people relate to themselves and to one another through either looking or being looked at. And that when we perceive ourselves being perceived we come to objectify ourselves in the same way we are being objectified. Thus, the gaze of the other robs us of our inherent freedom and causes us to deprive ourselves of our existence as a being-for-itself and instead learn to falsely self-identify as a being-in-itself. Therefore, under the gaze of the Other, our identities withdraw into the shell of reduction. We no more exist by ourselves, but our lives become entangled in the labyrinthine network of other people’s existence.<br />But, perhaps the “gaze”can best be understood in psychological terms: "to gaze implies more than to look at – it signifies a psychological relationship of power, in which the gazer is superior to the object of the gaze." The mere possible presence of another person causes one to look at oneself as an object and see one's world as it appears to the other. This is not done from a specific location outside oneself, but is non-positional. This is a recognition of the subjectivity in others. In fact, our perception within the field of the Other is therefore, always and forever, a battle with the Gaze, which casts its powerful net over one’s individuality, freedom and judgment, and fixes and defines them on its whim.<br />Sartre’s observation: “Hell is other people”, not only becomes then an amusing cocktail party quip, but also stands as a direct confirmation of the power that other people have over us. Other people literally become hell, a state where torture is not meted out by devils but self-inflicted and inescapable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16052096241991679986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sumirti Singaravel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5206467-sumirti-singaravel"><img alt="Sumirti Singaravel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580902981p2/5206467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1049379588?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sumirti Singaravel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sumirti Singaravel" href="/user/show/5206467-sumirti-singaravel">Sumirti Singaravel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=to-be-read-before-i-bid-adieu">to-be-read-before-i-bid-adieu</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=european-france">european-france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=amazing-work">amazing-work</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=small-and-beautiful">small-and-beautiful</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5206467-sumirti-singaravel?shelf=play">play</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1049379588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11450453642739976498"><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>"One always dies too soon—or too late. And yet one's whole life is complete at that moment[of death], with a line drawn neatly under it, ready for the summing up. You are—your life and nothing else."</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br/><br/>This is my first book by Jean-Paul Sartre and I thoroughly enjoyed it. The play in its entirety is based on the basic assertion of Sartre that "existence precedes essence". Each of the characters is complete in their development and is perfectly complementing to the central theme. I wonde</span>
  <span id="freeText11450453642739976498" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>"One always dies too soon—or too late. And yet one's whole life is complete at that moment[of death], with a line drawn neatly under it, ready for the summing up. You are—your life and nothing else."</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />This is my first book by Jean-Paul Sartre and I thoroughly enjoyed it. The play in its entirety is based on the basic assertion of Sartre that "existence precedes essence". Each of the characters is complete in their development and is perfectly complementing to the central theme. I wonder how Sartre made it possible for the reader to understand about the room in hell without ever giving an explanation of it. <br /><br />Each of the damned characters seeks the surety of their worth in the next person, like a mirror, rather than seeking comfort in their own judgement of themselves. Each of the characters fails to accept themselves and keeps on weaving a lie to themselves. It is most visible in Garcin's dilemma and his subsequent persuasion to Estelle to call him a courageous person and to put trust on him, and his inaction to leap out to the long corridor as soon as the door opened to escape to freedom. In their own weakness to be in harmony with themselves and to take responsibility for their own actions, they create a hell for one another. Thus, it gives rise to Garcin's words, the most famous of Sartre, <i>"Hell is other people."</i><br /><br />Another point of the play which made to think is the importance of Sleep in human's life. It is only in sleep, I realized after reading this play, that a man wholly belongs to himself. He remains uninterrupted and certainly without the necessity of another man's consciousness. <br />Perhaps, is that the reason why a good sound sleep makes us feel as if in paradise when we wake up?!<br /><br />I am looking forward eagerly to read more of Sartre. If not for anything, at least for the new ideas he gives away for racking my brain.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11450453642739976498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1808301720" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1808301720" />
    <a title="Sohaib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib"><img alt="Sohaib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599511226p2/31777090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1808301720?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sohaib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sohaib" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib">Sohaib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1808301720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17338546937789033023"><b>“Blinking, we call it. It’s like a small black shutter that clicks down and makes a break. Everything goes black; one’s eyes are moistened. You can’t imagine how restful, refreshing, it is. Four thousand little rests per hour. Four thousand little respites—just think!”</b><br/><br/>And here it is ladies and gentlemen: the theater of the absurd, the surreal, in which characters linger in a dream state only to reflect our own fickle and ungrounded reality.<br/><br/>We can’t appreciate the little things in life like “blin</span>
  <span id="freeText17338546937789033023" style="display:none"><b>“Blinking, we call it. It’s like a small black shutter that clicks down and makes a break. Everything goes black; one’s eyes are moistened. You can’t imagine how restful, refreshing, it is. Four thousand little rests per hour. Four thousand little respites—just think!”</b><br /><br />And here it is ladies and gentlemen: the theater of the absurd, the surreal, in which characters linger in a dream state only to reflect our own fickle and ungrounded reality.<br /><br />We can’t appreciate the little things in life like “blinking”—the intermittent rests from a continuous dreadful gaze at the world. We can’t appreciate the little things until we lose them. Yet these simple things in life are the ones we need most appreciate and hold onto.<br /><br />This play tells the story of three characters, a man and two women, forced to endure eternity with each other in a drawing room. They can’t blink. They can’t sleep. They can’t stop thinking. And they can’t stop gazing at one another. They’re only bound to endlessly torment each other. <br /><br />In a word, in this play, “Hell [becomes] other people!”</span>
  <a data-text-id="17338546937789033023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1942071959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7544550233979110834">I liked the concept, I liked the idea, I like a philosophical play, but literary speaking, this could be way more exciting if it was written by Albert Camus. I totally agree with the key line_HELL IS OTHER PEOPLE, but the writing wasn't breathtaking. I wish it was.<br/>Also the characters were kind of boring. They could have been different people and their dialogues could have made the reader produce more 'wow's and 'dammit's. I cannot help thinking this play is an unfulfilled idea, written by someon</span>
  <span id="freeText7544550233979110834" style="display:none">I liked the concept, I liked the idea, I like a philosophical play, but literary speaking, this could be way more exciting if it was written by Albert Camus. I totally agree with the key line_HELL IS OTHER PEOPLE, but the writing wasn't breathtaking. I wish it was.<br />Also the characters were kind of boring. They could have been different people and their dialogues could have made the reader produce more 'wow's and 'dammit's. I cannot help thinking this play is an unfulfilled idea, written by someone who is more of a thinker than a writer.</span>

<div id="review_1068260899" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1068260899" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1309716-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529984339p2/1309716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1068260899?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1309716-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<div id="review_3316822852" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3316822852" />
    <a title="André" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7215128-andr"><img alt="André" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586543083p2/7215128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3316822852?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="André" class="user" itemprop="url" name="André" href="/user/show/7215128-andr">André</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/45913602-no-exit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7215128-andr?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3316822852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1972842289209148628">Written in 1944, "No exist" is an existentialist play that takes place in an afterlife scene. Full of bright ironic remarks, Sartre's work conveys deep thoughts on the ontological sphere. <br/><br/>The story transports us to Hell, where three individuals (Joseph Garcin, Inèz Serrano, and Estelle Rigault) are brought to the same mysterious room. Hell is portrayed differently. It is not the classic environment with physical torture and burning charcoals, but a mere room with old-fashioned furniture. Initial</span>
  <span id="freeText1972842289209148628" style="display:none">Written in 1944, "No exist" is an existentialist play that takes place in an afterlife scene. Full of bright ironic remarks, Sartre's work conveys deep thoughts on the ontological sphere. <br /><br />The story transports us to Hell, where three individuals (Joseph Garcin, Inèz Serrano, and Estelle Rigault) are brought to the same mysterious room. Hell is portrayed differently. It is not the classic environment with physical torture and burning charcoals, but a mere room with old-fashioned furniture. Initially, the three damned souls are unaware of each other's crime, yet, they conclude that they were put in the same room to make each other miserable. The story starts to heat up when they decide to confess their crimes. Consequently, an ironic "triangle of love" starts to build among them.<br /><br />The three characters can't escape from the room. The main door is locked and a feeling of eternal despair grows among the three souls. The stories behind their crimes end up revealing their characters and their nature. Moreover, each one of them has a certain personality and a specific nature. Thus, ironically speaking, the three characters are entangled in a peculiar triangle of "love". Around this triangle, among the three, there are feelings/sentiments/emotions of love, hate, validation-seeking, jealously, indifference, neediness, and anger. For that reason, it's this connection that makes their torture much more severe. <br /> <br />All the characters reveal how despicable they are while they get to know each other. Those characters, after all, represent the worst of human nature. <br />They are aware of their flaws and their mistakes, but they haven't done anything to change during their lifetime. According to Sartre, if we are responsible for own freedom of choice, we are capable of changing our behaviours. So, perhaps, if the three souls are aware of their "devilish" attitude, the story implies that they chose to be like this, to be condemned that way. <br /><br />Sartre presents a simple but yet powerful story about the Human condition, such as free-will, bad faith and "alien freedom". Also, the witty ironic remarks make the play even more splendid. <br />"No exit" provides a magnificent existentialist story for the theatre format, which is undoubtedly a fabulous phenomenon by Jean-Paul Sartre.<br /><br />“Hell is—other people!”<br /><br />Rating: 4/5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="1972842289209148628" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_536801582" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/536801582" />
    <a title="Manohar Lal Solanki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15289660-manohar-lal-solanki"><img alt="Manohar Lal Solanki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354969701p2/15289660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/536801582?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manohar Lal Solanki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manohar Lal Solanki" href="/user/show/15289660-manohar-lal-solanki">Manohar Lal Solanki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer536801582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12032029579212182858">Jean-Paul Sartre, an existensialst, a philosopher, a novelist etc. His idea of hell is very much similar to the idea of Shakespeare. Shakespeare has quoted once, " All the devils of hell are on the earth". This novel goes on the same bottom line where he depicts a picture of hell, in which he shows three peoples who dies, and then they enters the hell. There were two ladies and one man. All the three peoples were kept in a same room. There was nothing else in the room except few paintings. There</span>
  <span id="freeText12032029579212182858" style="display:none">Jean-Paul Sartre, an existensialst, a philosopher, a novelist etc. His idea of hell is very much similar to the idea of Shakespeare. Shakespeare has quoted once, " All the devils of hell are on the earth". This novel goes on the same bottom line where he depicts a picture of hell, in which he shows three peoples who dies, and then they enters the hell. There were two ladies and one man. All the three peoples were kept in a same room. There was nothing else in the room except few paintings. There was a switch so that in case if they require any thing or any sort of assistance, then can buzz the alarm by switching, but it was not really working, so it was futile to have it. <br /><br />As all three introduces each other, and all three acknowledge that they were duped by people on earth about the concept and idea of hell. As they were expecting some sort of eternal purgatory where the King of hell will torment peoples with various freaky and vicious technique for their wrong done on the earth. It turns out to be totally different for them.<br /><br />In this novel, all the peoples begin conversation with each other by posing question to each other about their reason for in hell. As no one tells the truth, may be, it is human nature to hide. They all knew that none have put out the truth. This conversation leads to fight among the threes, finally man concludes that hell is not what has been told to us but hell is nothing but we people, we act as hell to each other, we make the life of others hell, as we don't have any exit to the room, it becomes hell for all three, novel ends with the raucous and fighting amongst all three.<br /><br />If you are a pro-pounder of Individualism, you will find this novel interesting as well as informative, in terms of how other peoples act as part of hell. <br /><br /><br />Read the book and enjoy, as it is a work of Jean-Paul-Sartre, one will find it more philosophical in content, hence one needs to read it as a good reader, it will require the reader to ponder on the words for few seconds as some time reader will find that it has the straight meaning the statement but it is true, his ideas make allusion various other collateral ideas which are necessary to understand. Give some time and read the book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12032029579212182858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
